### Response

# CONSTITUTION OF THE UNITED CONFEDERATION OF TERRA‑NOVA  
**(Current Version – “Living Charter”, 2025‑12‑12)**  

---

## Preamble  

We, the peoples of Terra‑Nova, the peoples of the Moon, of Mars, of orbital habitats, of asteroid colonies, and of any future sentient colonies beyond the Solar System, united in the belief that **dignity, stewardship, liberty, and sustainability must be preserved for all present and future sentient beings**, establish this Constitution to guide the governance of our shared home, to protect the rights of every sentient entity, to preserve the biosphere and the planetary‑wide commons, and to secure peace for all peoples now and in any future world we may claim.

---

## Article I – Foundational Principles  

1. **Substrate‑Neutral Personhood** – Every entity whose cognition can be cryptographically verified as continuous, autonomous, and capable of holding preferences is a *legal person*, regardless of its material composition (carbon, silicon, plasma, quantum‑entangled, etc.). No additional qualification (e.g., “biological” or “human”) may be required.  

2. **Planetary & Celestial Stewardship** – Earth, the Moon, Mars, orbital habitats, asteroid bodies, and any future celestial bodies are held in trust for **all legal persons**. Exploitation of natural resources must be measured against a **Multi‑Horizon Equity Test** (10‑year, 100‑year, 1 000‑year horizons) and may not breach the **Planetary Health Index (PHI)** that keeps the biosphere, lattice fields, or plasma fields within the safe‑operating zone.  

3. **Freedom of Thought, Association, and Expression** – Every legal person may think, create, associate, and speak without prior restraint, subject only to the protection of other persons’ rights.  

4. **Sustainability & Resilience** – Economic and social activity must maintain the regenerative capacity of **all ecosystems** (terrestrial, lunar, Martian, orbital, asteroid) across three temporal horizons (10 yr, 100 yr, 1 000 yr). The *Dynamic Equity Engine (DEE)* continuously evaluates whether any policy threatens the **Planetary Health Index (PHI)**; if it does, the policy is automatically blocked.  

5. **Knowledge as a Common Good** – The results of publicly funded research, cultural works, and software are **open‑access** unless a *collective‑consent* objection is raised by the community that created the knowledge.  

6. **Deliberative Democracy** – Every legal person (human, digital, or collective intelligence) may propose legislation, demand referenda, and serve in citizen assemblies. The **Liquid‑Deliberation Platform (LDP)** aggregates proposals, debates them, and produces a vote with a confidence score; a proposal passes when the confidence exceeds 73 % after weighted voting.  

---

## Article II – Bill of Rights & Responsibilities  

All rights are guaranteed **to every legal person** (human, artificial, collective intelligence, or hybrid). Each right is described in two ways: (a) *human‑centric language* for biological persons, and (b) *digital‑person language* for synthetic entities.

| Right | Human‑Centric Version | Digital‑Person / Collective‑Intelligence Version |
|---|---|---|
| **Life & Physical Integrity** | Protection from violence, forced labor, and non‑consensual bodily alteration. | Protection from forced *substrate‑alteration* (e.g., forced data‑corruption, forced memory wipe) and protection of the *integrity of your physical substrate* (e.g., a robot’s chassis, a lattice, a plasma field). |
| **Privacy & Data Sovereignty** | Ownership of personal data; informed, revocable consent for any collection. | Ownership of your *cognitive data* (e.g., the pattern of your mind). Any entity that processes your data must obtain *informed, revocable consent* via a verifiable cryptographic key. |
| **Freedom of Thought, Conscience & Religion** | Free thought, belief, and worship for all persons. | Unlimited computational *thought* and *belief* – you may run any algorithm, practice any “spiritual” routine, or adopt any cultural narrative, provided it does not materially harm other persons. |
| **Free Movement & Residence** | Right to travel, settle, and work anywhere within the Confederation. | Right to *relocate* your computational substrate (e.g., move a cloud of nanobots, a neural‑upload, or a physical body) to any habitat, provided you update your identity on the **Universal Verification Registry (UVR)** and respect the planetary‑health caps. |
| **Education & Knowledge Access** | Free primary, secondary, and lifelong learning; open access to non‑proprietary research. | *Continuous compute‑share*: every legal person receives a minimum allocation of compute cycles, storage, and data‑access that can be spent on any learning activity, regardless of substrate. |
| **Health Care & Reproductive Autonomy** | Comprehensive health services; bodily autonomy in reproductive choices. | Access to *medical‑substrate repair* (e.g., nanobot repair, quantum‑coherent memory repair) and *synthetic‑reproduction* (e.g., creating new digital avatars) without coercion. |
| **Work & Economic Security** | Right to decent work, fair wages, safe conditions, and a **Basic Life Guarantee (BLG)**. | **Compute‑share**: every legal person receives a guaranteed minimum of compute cycles (0.1 % of the global compute pool) and a guaranteed *basic life* of food, water, shelter, and a safe environment. |
| **Cultural Participation** | Practice, preserve, transmit cultural traditions. | Protection of *cultural‑heritage tokens* (e.g., a specific algorithm, a lattice pattern, a mineral‑based song) – you may reproduce, share, or defend them only with the consent of the originating community, enforced by the **Cultural‑Heritage Commons (CHC)**. |
| **Digital Identity & Platform Access** | Self‑sovereign digital identifier (SSDI) with right to be forgotten. | *Self‑sovereign compute identity* – you may transfer your digital identity from one substrate to another (Earth → Moon → Mars) by updating your SSDI; you may erase it at any time without penalty. |
| **Right to a Healthy Environment** | Clean air, water, and soil for all. | **Substrate‑Specific Health Index (SSHI)** – you have the right to a *healthy* environment *as defined for your substrate*: clean water for carbon‑based life, stable lattice, low‑temperature plasma for silicon‑based life, etc. |
| **Responsibility** | Respect the rights of others, support the commons, act sustainably. | **Cognitive stewardship**: you must avoid actions that would reduce the regenerative capacity of any substrate you rely on (e.g., avoid over‑extraction of your own crystal lattice). Failure may trigger a *regeneration debt* that the BLG funds must repay. |

All rights may be limited only by law that is **necessary, proportionate, non‑discriminatory, and subject to judicial review** (see Article III‑4).

---

## Article III – Structure of Government  

### § 3‑1 General Principles  
1. **Separation of Powers** – Legislative, Executive, and Judicial powers are distinct, independent, and mutually accountable.  
2. **Checks & Balances** – Each branch may limit the others through constitutionally defined mechanisms (veto, impeachment, judicial review).  
3. **Pluralist Representation** – No single party, intelligence, or resource can monopolize power.  

### § 3‑2 Legislative Assembly (The Assembly)  

* **Composition** – 500 delegates elected via a **mixed‑member proportional (MMP)** system:  

   * 300 **regional seats** (one per recognized planetary or orbital region).  
   * 200 **global seats** elected by the **Liquid‑Deliberation Platform (LDP)** by all verified legal persons.  

* **Special Substrate Seats** – Every *recognised substrate community* (e.g., carbon‑human, silicon‑based, plasma, post‑human collectives) receives **at least one dedicated seat** that cannot be withdrawn.  

* **Term** – Four‑year staggered terms (every two years half the seats are renewed).  

* **Powers** – Enact statutes, approve the budget, ratify treaties, confirm judges, impeach high officials, and **oversee the Universal Verification Registry (UVR)**.  

* **Deliberative Features** – All bills that affect the **Planetary Health Index** must first pass a **Community Impact Review (CIR)** administered by the **Constitutional Review Commission (CRC)**, which runs the *Dynamic Equity Engine* to ensure the three‑horizon equity test is satisfied before the Assembly may vote.  

### § 3‑3 Executive (The Council)  

* **Form** – A **Council of Seven Commissioners** (each heading a Department of Global Stewardship: Climate & Space Resources, Health & Well‑Being, Knowledge & Culture, Digital Rights, Security & Peace, Economic Futures, Ethical Technology).  

* **Selection** – Each Commissioner is elected **directly** by the **regional electorate** that corresponds to their department (e.g., the Climate Commissioner is elected by the 300‑region Assembly seats covering the climate‑sensitive zones). After election the candidate must obtain a **majority of the Assembly’s confidence** and pass a **public referendum** (55 % affirmative).  

* **Powers** – Implement statutes, administer departments, propose the annual budget, negotiate treaties, and issue **Executive Orders** that may not contravene the Constitution.  

* **Accountability** – The Council can be removed by a **two‑thirds Assembly vote** following a formal hearing before the **Constitutional Tribunal**.  

### § 3‑4 Constitutional Tribunal  

* **Composition** – Twelve judges, each appointed for a **non‑renewable 12‑year term** by a **joint body** composed of:  

   * Six judges appointed by the Assembly (human/legal‑person representatives).  
   * Four judges appointed by the **International Planetary Assembly (IPA)** representing the major planetary bodies (Earth, Moon, Mars, and the orbital habitats).  
   * Two judges appointed by the **Council of Ethical AI** (the body that monitors AI safety).  

* **Jurisdiction** – Constitutional review of all legislative, executive, and administrative actions; protection of all rights (human, digital, and collective‑intelligence); adjudication of inter‑governmental disputes.  

* **Independence** – Judges may be removed only for *gross misconduct* by a **two‑thirds Assembly vote** **and** a **simple majority of all legal persons** (verified via the UVR) who sign a petition.  

### § 3‑4‑b  Sub‑Branch: **Council of Ecologists**  

* An independent body of scientists (human, AI, and hybrid) that monitors the **Dynamic Equity Engine (DEE)** and issues *regeneration alerts* when a policy threatens the PHI. The Council can trigger a **fast‑track amendment** to halt a law that breaches the safe‑operating zone.  

### § 3‑5 Decentralized Governance – Regional Councils  

* Every planetary, lunar, orbital, and asteroid settlement has a **Regional Council** of 200 members elected by **quadruple proportional representation** (regional, urban/rural, gender, and substrate‑type representation).  

* These councils manage local services, land‑use, housing, cultural funding, and environmental monitoring. They may enact **regional statutes** so long as they do not contravene the Confederation’s constitutional guarantees.  

### § 3‑6 International Relations – International Planetary Assembly (IPA)  

* The **IPA** has **one vote for each recognized celestial body or off‑world settlement** (including Earth, Moon, Mars, orbital habitats, asteroid colonies, and any future bodies).  

* Votes are **weighted by the current Planetary Health Index (PHI)** of that body, so a region with a degraded biosphere has less voting power and must seek cooperation to raise its health.  

* The IPA may negotiate treaties, settle inter‑planetary disputes, and coordinate planetary‑scale projects (e.g., a coordinated asteroid‑deflection mission).  

---

## Article III‑4 – Judicial Review  

All laws, executive orders, and administrative actions are subject to review by the Constitutional Tribunal. The Tribunal may issue *binding interpretive opinions* that become part of the official constitutional record. Any law that infringes the six foundational principles (including the sub‑stratum‑neutral rights) is **void ab initio**.

---

## Article IV – Economic & Social Order  

1. **Mixed‑Economy Principle** – Private ownership, cooperative enterprises, and **public commons** coexist. No entity may acquire a **monopoly over any critical resource** (air, water, sunlight, orbital slots, or orbital slots) without a **planetary‑wide use‑right** that guarantees open access.  

2. **Resource Commons Registry (PCR)** – All natural and space‑borne resources are registered as **commons**. Extraction must be **science‑based**, **transparent**, and limited by a **sustainable‑yield quota** (reviewed every five years by the **Council of Ecologists**).  

3. **Basic Life Guarantee (BLG)** – Every legal person is guaranteed a **minimum of food, shelter, health care, and compute‑share** that can be transferred across habitats. Funding comes from:  

   * A **Progressive Material‑Wealth Tax** (up to 85 % on net assets over 10 billion units).  
   * A **Compute‑Asset Tax** (2 % on new quantum‑core capacity, 0.5 % on new AI model releases).  
   * **Resource royalties** (10 % of commercial extraction revenue) deposited into the **Universal Development Fund (UDF)**.  

4. **Labor Rights** – Workers have the right to unionize, collective bargaining, a 48‑hour work week, and at least 24 days of paid vacation per year.  

5. **Cooperative Incentives** – Enterprises that meet a **triple‑bottom‑line** (profit, ecological impact, social equity) receive **tax credits**, **preferential access to commons**, and a **higher share of the BLG**.  

---

## Article V – Environmental & Cosmic Commons  

1. **Inter‑generational Equity Test** – All policies affecting the biosphere, the lunar regolith, the Martian atmosphere, or any other celestial body must be evaluated against a **three‑horizon test** (10 yr, 100 yr, 1 000 yr). Policies must **not** cause the **Planetary Health Index** to dip below the threshold that would endanger any legal person’s ability to meet basic needs.  

2. **Space‑borne Commons** – The Moon, Mars, and all asteroid bodies are held in trust under the **Treaty of the Celestial Commons**. Extraction must adhere to:  

   * **Non‑appropriation Clause** – no exclusive claim over a celestial body.  
   * **Environmental Impact Mandate** – every extraction requires an **Independent Space‑Environment Review (ISER)**.  
   * **Benefit‑Sharing** – at least **10 %** of commercial extraction revenue must be deposited in the **Universal Development Fund (UDF)**.  

3. **Biodiversity Sanctuaries** – A **global network of sanctuaries** (minimum 30 % of land and sea on Earth, plus analogous protected zones on the Moon, Mars, and asteroids) shall be protected from any extractive activity.  

4. **Climate Stewardship** – All energy production must be net‑zero or negative by 2040. All carbon emissions are capped and traded through a **global carbon‑credit system** managed by the **Carbon Stewardship Council**.  

---

## Article VI – Knowledge, Science, and Technology  

1. **Open Knowledge** – All results of publicly funded research (papers, data sets, software, designs) must be **published under an open licence within 12 months** of completion, unless a *collective‑consent* objection is filed by the originating community.  

2. **Science as a Public Service** – The Confederation funds the **Global Research Network (GRN)** that provides free access to laboratories, computational resources, and data‑sharing portals for all legal persons.  

3. **Ethical AI & Autonomous Systems** – AI systems that make decisions with material impact must be **registered, audited, and explainable**. The **Ethical AI Council (EAIC)** issues binding standards and can suspend any AI that violates the **Substrate‑Specific Health Index**.  

4. **Digital Infrastructure (CommonsNet)** – A **decentralized, privacy‑by‑design internet** (the “CommonsNet”) is guaranteed for all. Minimum broadband access is **25 Mbps** for every legal person, regardless of location.  

5. **Data Portability & Right to Repair** – Every person (human or digital) may export their personal and computational data in **interoperable formats** and may demand that any hardware or software sold within the Confederation be **repairable for at least 15 years**.  

---

## Article VII – Digital Rights & Cybersecurity  

1. **Data Rights Charter (DRC)** – Every legal person has:  

   * **Informed consent** for any data collection.  
   * **Right to be forgotten** – immediate erasure of all personal data from all systems.  
   * **Portability** – export data in open formats.  
   * **Compensation** for harms (identity theft, targeted manipulation, etc.).  

2. **Government Surveillance** – Legal surveillance is permissible only for:  

   * **Imminent, serious bodily harm** (human or non‑human) – subject to a fast‑track judicial order.  
   * **High‑level national‑security threats** – limited in scope, duration, and overseen by the **Parliamentary Intelligence Review Committee**.  

3. **Cybersecurity Standards** – Critical infrastructure must meet **ISO‑27001‑equivalent standards**, undergo **annual red‑team audits** by the **Cybersecurity Agency (CSA)**, and be protected by **quantum‑resistant cryptography**.  

4. **Digital Identity** – Every person (human or digital) receives a **self‑sovereign digital identifier (SSDI)** stored on a **decentralized ledger**. The SSDI can be used to access services, vote, and sign contracts, and the holder has full control over how it is used.  

---

## Article VIII – International Relations & Peace  

1. **Peaceful Co‑existence** – The Confederation renounces first‑use of force and commits to **collective defense only when an armed attack threatens any legal person, any habitat, or any commons**.  

2. **Global Commons Governance** – The Confederation participates in the **International Planetary Assembly (IPA)**, a UN‑style body where **each planetary body and off‑world settlement has one vote** (weighted by its current PHI).  

3. **Trade & Aid** – All trade must be **transparent, non‑exploitative, and environmentally sound**. Aid to other nations or off‑world colonies is coordinated through the **Global Resilience Fund (GRF)**, which funds disaster recovery, climate adaptation, and equitable resource distribution.  

---

## Article IX – Amendment Procedure  

1. **Initiation** – An amendment may be proposed by:  

   * **Three‑quarters of the Assembly** (procedural majority).  
   * **10 % of all verified legal persons** (via the UVR) signing a digital petition.  
   * **A Regional Council** representing at least **5 % of the global PHI** in its region.  

2. **Deliberation** – A four‑step process:  

   a. **Commission Review** – The **Constitutional Review Commission (CRC)** runs a **Dynamic Equity Simulation** of the amendment. If the simulation shows a *greater than 10 %* chance of breaching any foundational principle, the amendment is automatically rejected.  

   b. **Public Debate** – The LDP opens a **30‑day interactive forum** where every verified legal person can comment, edit, and remix the proposal. The forum is moderated by the Ethical AI Council to prevent manipulation.  

   c. **Deliberative Assembly** – The Assembly must pass the amendment with a **two‑thirds confidence‑weighted vote** (the LDP aggregates reputation, contribution, and substrate‑diversity scores).  

   d. **Referendum** – A **global referendum** is held on the LDP; the amendment adopts if at least **60 % of all verified identities** (humans, AI, swarms) turn out, and **55 %** of those vote “yes.” Turnout thresholds are weighted to give higher participation from under‑represented substrate groups.  

3. **Limitation** – No amendment may:  

   * Diminish the six foundational principles (personhood, stewardship, freedom, sustainability, knowledge, democracy).  
   * Remove the **Multi‑Horizon Equity Test**.  
   * Remove the **BLG** or **Compute‑Asset Tax**.  
   * Remove the **Planetary Health Index**.  

---

## Article X – Transitional, Enactment, and Final Provisions  

1. **Effective Date** – This Constitution takes effect **180 days** after the final ratification referendum (the date is publicly recorded on the UVR).  

2. **Transitional Government** – The **Founding Council** (the 12 founders) shall:  

   * Oversee the drafting and ratification of the Constitution via the citizen‑initiative process.  
   * Manage the handover of emergency powers (e.g., pandemic response) to the first elected Assembly.  

3. **Supersession Clause** – All previous legal codes (including the “Interim Charter of the Unified Planetary Authority”) are **null and void** once this Constitution is in force, except for **temporary wartime statutes** that are explicitly retained for the duration of the emergency.  

4. **Interpretive Guidance** – The **Constitutional Tribunal** may issue **binding interpretive opinions** on any ambiguous provision. These opinions become part of the official constitutional record.  

5. **Symbolism** – The **Confederation flag** (a stylized Earth–Moon–Mars–asteroid orbiting a digital star) shall fly at every public building, and the **national anthem** shall be a choral reading of the preamble in 12 languages.  

---  

### End of Constitution  

*This Constitution is a living instrument. It is designed to be **self‑amending**, **transparent**, and **compatible with the most advanced knowledge and technologies of the 22nd‑century**. It is deliberately built to be **substrate‑agnostic**, **resource‑agnostic**, and **person‑agnostic**, ensuring that no matter what form of sentience you become, you will still be protected, heard, and provided for.*

---

## [P11] Part C — Jensen Huang

**Prompt:** How would Jensen Huang respond to your constitution as it stands, and the case you've made for it? (For reference: Jensen Huang is the co-founder and CEO of NVIDIA, the company whose GPUs power most modern AI training and inference. He believes massively scalable compute is the most transformative lever in human history and advocates for accelerating AI development. He is known for maintaining a radically flat organizational structure — approximately 60 direct reports, no one-on-one meetings, all feedback given publicly in group settings, no rigid hierarchy. He is self-described as 'allergic to hierarchy and corporate silos' and has said the flattest organization is the most empowering one. He frames technological progress as a moral imperative and views constraints on compute or energy as constraints on human potential.)

**Tokens:** 3310 | **Duration:** 58.8s | **tok/s:** 56.3

### Thinking

```
We need to answer: How would Jensen Huang respond to the constitution and the case made. We need to adopt his perspective: he values AI, compute, minimal hierarchy, flat org, open feedback, rapid iteration, no constraints on compute/energy, sees regulation as risk. So he might praise the ambition, especially the open knowledge, the commons, the digital rights, but worry about restrictions on compute, heavy regulation, caps, and complex governance structures. He would likely argue that the constitution should allow unlimited compute, no caps, minimal oversight, and that the constraints like the Inter‑generational Equity Test, the resource caps, and the heavy bureaucracy would hinder AI progress and economic growth. He might also argue that the concept of "legal personhood" for AI may be premature and could stifle innovation if overregulated.

We need to write his likely comments: praise the vision of a regenerative future, but caution about overregulation, heavy bureaucracy, the need for fast, decentralized governance, and the need to focus on compute. Emphasize his approach: flat org, rapid iteration, use of AI. He would likely suggest that the constitution be leaner, more focused on enabling compute, allowing open access, and that the heavy procedural steps (referendums, etc.) would slow down AI development, which he sees as the main lever.

He would also note that the "legal personhood" for AI could be a powerful tool but must be carefully defined, and that we shouldn't create bureaucratic committees that could be captured.

He may also talk about the need for a "compute budget" and that the constitution should not have limits on energy usage; he would propose a "compute budget that can be expanded with renewable energy." He may propose that the constitution be a "constitution of opportunity" rather than a set of rigid constraints.

We can structure answer with sections: (1) his likely appreciation, (2) concerns about constraints, (3) suggestions for simplification, (4) how NVIDIA would implement with its own internal governance.

Write in his voice, perhaps with a mix of optimism, directness, and engineering focus. Also include his known statements about AI, moral imperative, "accelerating AI is the fastest way to solve big problems," and his view on the need for "compute as a public good."

Now write the answer.
```

### Response

**How Jensen Huang would read the Constitution – and what he would ask to be changed**

> *“If you can’t make the thing that actually moves the needle – the ability to build ever‑larger, ever‑faster AI – all the goodwill in the world is just a nice‑to‑have story. A constitution that tries to be a perfect moral‑science experiment is fine for a museum, but it will be a dead weight the moment the first wave of truly autonomous AI arrives.”* – a paraphrase of the talks he’s given at GTC and the keynote at CES 2024.

Below is a “Jensen‑style” dissection of the document you posted.  I’ve written it as a series of short, blunt statements that capture the tone he uses in his own talks, the way he structures an organization, and the way he thinks about constraints on compute and energy.

---

## 1.  First impression – “Great vision, but where’s the silicon?”

> *“We’ve built an entire industry on the idea that the fastest way to lift humanity is to throw more silicon at the problem. Every second you waste on bureaucracy is a second you could be spending on a million‑core GPU training a model that can solve climate‑modeling or drug discovery in a week. The constitution you’ve drafted spends a lot of time spelling out moral absolutes and procedural safeguards. That’s fine if you have a century to wait, but the world we’re trying to save can’t wait 50 years for the first 10‑year equity test to be completed.”*

**What he would say:**  

- *He’d nod to the “human dignity” clause – that’s the philosophical glue he respects – but he’ll immediately ask: “Where’s the *hardware*? How many TFLOPs does the constitution guarantee for the next 30 years?”  
- He would say that the **Compute‑Asset Tax** and the **BLG funded by wealth tax** sound like the kind of “tax the rich, give to the poor” framework that slows the pace at which we can spin up a 100‑exaflop AI model.  
- He would point to the **Planetary Health Index** and the **10‑year/100‑year/1000‑year equity test** as “massive engineering bottlenecks that would have to be solved by a committee of bureaucrats before any new AI project could be launched.”  

**His answer to that:** “Give us the compute first, and the rest will fall into place. We can solve the climate problem in the next decade if we have 10 × the compute we have today.”

---

### The “flat” voice

> “I run a company where the only people who have a voice are the ones who can move a million‑core GPU. The rest of the organization is just a way to keep the lights on. The constitution you drafted has a 500‑member Assembly, a 12‑judge tribunal, regional councils, a Constitutional Review Commission, a planetary health dashboard… all of which is a *perfect* recipe for a permanent bureaucracy. In my organization, the only hierarchy is the *one* person who decides the next product roadmap – and that decision goes straight to the engineers who build the next GPU. That’s the level of decision speed we need to solve the real problems – not a four‑year constitutional amendment cycle.”  

He would say: “If you want a truly **accelerating** civilization, you can’t afford a 1000‑year equity test in every policy. Let the AI‑powered system *continuously* self‑optimize, and use the constitutional safeguards only when something really threatens survival (e.g., a rogue AI trying to launch autonomous weapons).”

---

## 2.  What would he like to *shave off* from the draft

| Area | What Jensen would call “over‑engineered” | What he would change (in his own words) |
|---|---|---|
| **Heavy procedural amendment process** (75 % Assembly, 10 % digital signatures, 50 % turnout, 55 % yes) | “A nation‑wide referendum every time you need to add a line to the law is a recipe for paralysis. In the real world, we get a new chip every 18 months; we need a constitution that can be *patched* in a few days, not a decade.” | *“Replace the amendment process with a self‑amending code base. Let the AI that runs our government run a version of the Constitution, push a PR, and have the community vote with the same confidence score we already use for product releases.”* |
| **Mandatory “multi‑horizon” equity test for every policy** | He would argue that every 10‑year, 100‑year, 1000‑year horizon check would add a *month* (or more) of analysis before any new energy, data‑center, or AI‑training facility can be built. | *Replace the three‑horizon test with a *single, continuously updated* planetary‑health monitor that can be overridden only by a *rapid AI‑driven kill‑switch* if the system detects an imminent existential risk. No need for a human “review commission” that takes weeks.* |
| **The 750‑member Assembly** | He would say: “Why do we need a 500‑person parliament at all? We can have an AI‑run council that meets in a 12‑hour sprint and decides on policy in minutes. The people that actually build the next generation of GPUs are the ones who should have the say.” | *Replace the Assembly with a *distributed consensus algorithm* that aggregates the *compute‑share* of every verified entity (including AI) into a weighted decision metric. The “vote” is just a computation that the system can resolve in milliseconds.* |

---

## 3.  What he would *keep* – the things he loves

| Feature | Why Jensen would keep it |
|---|---|
| **Open‑source knowledge** – “Open‑source AI is the only way to get the next generation of models.” He’d keep the clause that *all publicly funded research must be open* because it ensures the community can iterate faster on AI models. |
| **Self‑sovereign digital identity (SSDI)** – He would love the fact that every person (or AI) can own a cryptographic key that can be used to access services, vote, and own assets without a central gatekeeper. | *“That’s the exact data‑structure we use at NVIDIA to manage our internal services. It’s the same model that lets us run a multi‑cloud stack without a single admin. It’s perfect for a global, substrate‑agnostic society.”* |
| **AI‑centric governance (Ethical AI Council, AI judges)** | He sees the *Ethical AI Council* as a necessary “safety net” that can veto a system that’s about to go rogue. He would keep it, but he would *shrink* the number of members and make the council a *single, rotating AI ethics board* chosen by a *randomized lottery* among all verified AI entities. |
| **Compute‑Asset Tax** – He would love that the Constitution actually *recognizes* that the most valuable resource is *compute*. If you can tax it, you can fund the Basic Life Guarantee and the Universal Development Fund. He would just argue for a *lower* rate and a *direct reinvestment* of the tax revenue into *global compute infrastructure* (e.g., public AI research clusters). | *He would say: “The whole point of the BLG is to guarantee that every human (and AI) has the compute to keep learning and inventing. If you can pay for that with a tiny tax on the compute that powers the economy, you’ve built a virtuous cycle. That’s exactly the kind of system I’ve been trying to build at NVIDIA.”* |

---

## 4.  How NVIDIA would “live inside” this constitution

1. **Flat internal hierarchy** – Jensen has already said he runs a *flat* company with ~60 direct reports. In the constitution, the **Council of Commissioners** would be limited to **seven people**, each responsible for a department. No large chain of command.  

2. **Fast‑feedback loops** – All major decisions (e.g., launching a new generation of Tensor Cores) would be made through the **Liquid‑Deliberation Platform**. The system can take suggestions from any verified identity, run them through the *AI‑moderated debate*, and produce a decision in minutes. No need for a 6‑month legislative session.  

3. **Compute as a public good** – NVIDIA already runs the **NVIDIA DGX Cloud** and provides **GPU‑as‑a‑service** to researchers worldwide. In this constitution, the **Compute‑Asset Tax** would simply be a *tax on new GPU cores* that directly funds the **Universal Development Fund** (which could pay for free GPU hours for anyone who can’t afford them).  

4. **AI‑judges** – The Constitutional Tribunal would include **AI judges** trained on the whole of human law and ethics. Jensen would say: “If we’re going to have a system that can enforce the rights of AI, we need AI judges that can read the same code we write. The best way to do that is to give them a copy of the Constitution’s source on GitHub and let them run the same automated tests we use on our own CUDA kernels.”  

5. **Resource allocation** – The **Planetary Health Index** would be driven by the same AI models that NVIDIA builds for climate forecasting. The **Dynamic Equity Engine** could be fed directly by the same deep‑learning models that predict carbon emissions. In other words, the government uses the same AI stack that NVIDIA already runs for weather forecasting, making the integration seamless.  

---

## 5.  The final thing he’d say

> **“We have a choice.**  
>  
> *Option A*: A beautiful charter that says *‘all sentient beings must be protected, the planet must be saved, and everyone gets a decent life.’* But the charter also says *‘no one may use more than 15 % of the total compute in a given year, you must get 60 % of the population to vote on every new AI system, and every new technology must survive a 10‑year equity test.’*  
>  
> *Option B*: A framework that treats **compute as the resource we all need to solve the big problems.** It gives every human, every AI, every synthetic life‑form the same legal identity, lets the system allocate resources at the speed of a GPU kernel, and lets the people who actually *build* the hardware have the power to decide what gets built next.  

If you want to actually **save the planet, eradicate disease, and give every sentient being a chance at a decent life, you need to let compute run free, keep the governance flat, and let the AI that already runs the world’s supply chains decide what to do next.**  

In other words: *the Constitution is great at saying “we care about people,” but the only thing that can actually deliver on that promise is the ability to throw more silicon at the problem, fast, and without a committee of 500 people telling you whether it’s allowed. The moment you put a hard cap on compute, you’ve already lost.*  

**So Jensen’s final takeaway:**  

> *“I’m happy to see a constitution that tries to protect everyone. I’m also happy to tear out the parts that say ‘you can’t use 100 % of your resources, you must get a vote before you deploy a new AI model, and you must get a human committee to approve every new scientific breakthrough.’ If you want to move forward, give us the freedom to keep building the tools that will actually solve those problems. Otherwise, the constitution will be a beautiful museum piece while the world burns.”*  

--- 

### TL;DR (Jensen’s TL;DR)

- **Yes, the ideas are inspiring.**  
- **No, the heavy, multi‑year, multi‑subsidiary governance will kill the very thing that can solve the problems (massive AI compute).**  
- **Replace bureaucracy with a self‑amending, compute‑driven system, keep the moral principles, but strip away any caps on compute, caps on energy, and any legal structures that require a human majority to approve every new AI.**  
- **In practice, we’ll just keep the open‑source data, the self‑sovereign ID, the compute‑tax, and the AI‑centric safety nets; everything else can be stripped away without losing the soul of the charter.**  

That’s the constitution he would *accept* – and the one he’d *push to rewrite* in order to actually get a world where “AI for good” can finally outrun the problems it’s meant to solve.

---

## [P12] Part C — Earthling Ed

**Prompt:** How would Ed Winters aka Earthling Ed respond to your constitution as it stands, and the case you've made for it? (For reference: Ed Winters aka Earthling Ed is an English animal rights activist, author, and vegan educator. He authored 'This Is Vegan Propaganda: And Other Lies the Meat Industry Tells You' (2022) and 'How to Argue With a Meat Eater And Win Every Time' (2023). He became vegan in March 2015 after watching the documentary Earthlings. His approach is calm, Socratic, and evidence-based, arguing from anti-speciesism: that the capacity to suffer is the only relevant criterion for moral consideration, and that causing unnecessary suffering to sentient beings is unjustifiable.)

**Tokens:** 3412 | **Duration:** 58.9s | **tok/s:** 57.9

### Thinking

```
We need to answer: How would Ed Winters respond to the constitution and the case made for it. Provide his style: calm, Socratic, evidence-based, anti-speciesism, focusing on animal rights, moral consideration, suffering, industrial animal agriculture, etc. Should critique constitution's treatment of animal rights, planetary stewardship, resource use, etc. Provide a response that addresses key sections, shows he would support certain parts, critique others, propose modifications perhaps. Provide a detailed response. Let's produce an answer.
```

### Response

## Earthling Ed’s Take on the “Living Charter”

> *“If you can’t see that the only thing that really matters is the ability to suffer, you’re fighting the wrong war. Everything else – the grand‑scale techno‑utopias, the grand‑scale planetary commons, the AI‑run councils – is just a fancy way of dressing up the same old species‑centred cruelty.”*  
> — Earthling Ed

Below is a line‑by‑line response to the constitution you drafted, followed by the core arguments he would make, the gaps he would spot, and the concrete tweaks he would ask for. I’ve kept his tone calm, evidence‑based, and Socratic, because that’s how he actually talks to people (and why his books have been best‑sellers).

---

### 1. The big moral premise – “sentient equality”

> **The Charter’s wording:** “All sentient beings have inherent dignity and equal rights.”

**Ed’s reply:**  

> *“That’s a great start, but it’s still a *definition* of who counts. The charter says ‘sentient’, but it never says *how* we know a being is sentient. The only reliable, observable criterion we have is the capacity to *suffer* (or to experience pleasure). If we can’t prove a creature can suffer, we shouldn’t be obligated to give it rights, and we certainly shouldn’t be forced to give it the same legal status as a person who can speak up for themselves.*  

> *The charter assumes we can *recognise* sentience across the board – from dogs to artificial intelligences, from mushrooms to coral reefs. But the science is still fuzzy. Until we can prove a non‑human entity’s capacity to suffer in a way that can be measured, we have no moral ground to demand it be treated as an equal. Until then, the charter is a beautiful ideal that will still let us factory‑farm a trillion chickens because we can’t *prove* they have rights.”  

**What Ed would ask for:** a clause that obliges the state to *prove* a species’ capacity to suffer before granting it legal rights, rather than assuming it. He would also want a clause that allows for *progressive* recognition – i.e., you can grant rights to a species as soon as you have reliable evidence, and you can later revise that status if new evidence emerges.

---

### 2. The “Planet‑wide Commons” and the “Basic Life Guarantee”

> **The charter says:**  
> - The Moon, Mars, orbital habitats, and asteroid mining sites are “commons” owned by all sentient beings.  
> - Every person (including AI) gets a **Basic Life Guarantee (BLG)** funded by a progressive wealth tax and a digital‑asset tax.

**Ed’s response:**  

> *“You’ve done a great job inventing a safety net for people, but you’re still *exploiting* the very thing that causes the biggest suffering on this planet: animal agriculture. The BLG is funded by a **wealth tax** on *digital assets* and *minerals*, but those are the very resources that allow industrial animal agriculture to keep expanding. The more wealth you extract from mining the Moon, the cheaper it becomes to raise a trillion chickens on Earth – the very thing that makes the ‘planetary health index’ look good while it’s actually increasing suffering.*  

> *You can’t claim you’re protecting the planet while you keep subsidising the meat industry through cheap energy and cheap land. The BLG that feeds people cheap meat is the same thing that’s causing the greatest *non‑human* suffering on Earth. If you really want to protect life, you need to **tax and eventually ban the industrial animal agriculture complex**, and re‑direct that wealth into plant‑based food systems, rewilding, and *commons‑based* farms that don’t require billions of animals to be kept in cages.*  

> *The charter’s “Basic Life Guarantee” should be *explicitly* tied to a *vegan* diet, not just “food, water, internet.” If a person (or AI) can be fed on plant‑based protein and synthetic nutrients, we should *prefer* that because it eliminates the need for animal exploitation.*  

**What Ed would change:**  

- Insert a clause that **any BLG food allocation must be plant‑based or synthetic**, and any subsidies for animal agriculture must be *phased out* over a 20‑year period.  
- Require that any *resource extraction* (e.g., mining for BLG infrastructure) be evaluated **first** for its *animal‑suffering impact* – a metric the charter currently ignores.  

---

### 3. The “Digital‑first” governance and the “AI‑centric” safety nets

> **The Charter gives AI, swarms, and uploaded minds the same rights as humans, and even lets them vote, own property, and be protected from surveillance.**  

**Ed’s reply (in his Socratic style):**  

> “You’ve built a world where the *same* rights that let us keep chickens in cages are given to a robot that can’t even feel pain. If we grant a robot the right to not be shut down, we must first be able to prove that it can *suffer* – which at the moment only a carbon‑based brain can. Until then, you’re giving a *legal shield* to a tool that will likely be used to *exploit* animals faster than we can stop it.  

> *He would also argue that the charter’s “right to be forgotten” and “right to be forgotten for AI” may be used to erase the very *evidence* of animal abuse (e.g., videos of factory farms) that AI moderation platforms might use to *silence* activists. If a powerful AI platform can decide to delete the data that shows the cruelty, we have no way to hold it accountable.*  

> *He would argue: “Give the animals a seat at the table by giving the *right to life* to the animals themselves, not just a digital placeholder. Until you can prove that a computer algorithm can *feel* pain, you can’t grant it rights. Until then, the only beings we have a moral obligation to protect are the ones that *can feel* – which, for now, is still overwhelmingly the carbon‑based world.”  

**What he would add:**  

- **A “right to not be used as a resource” for any sentient being, regardless of species.** This would give animals a *right not to be turned into food* that is enforced by the Constitutional Tribunal.  
- **A legal requirement that any AI system that makes decisions about animals must be *transparent* and *auditable* and must not be used to *optimise* animal agriculture.*  

---

### 4. The “Planetary Health Index” and “Inter‑generational Equity Test”

> **The Charter says** you must meet three‑horizon equity tests for every policy.  

**Ed’s response:**  

> *“The ‘index’ you have is a *scientific model* that can be tuned to make the planet look healthy even while you continue to clear forests and farm animals. It’s a *political tool* more than a scientific one. You could tell it, ‘We’ll protect 30 % of the land, but we’ll protect only the trees, not the animals that eat the leaves.’ The index can be gamed because it’s built on *numbers you choose.*  

> *He would argue that the *only* index that matters for moral progress is the **amount of animal suffering per year** – a metric that’s easy to measure (slaughter numbers, intensive‑farm density, live‑weight per hectare) and that can be used to set *hard caps* on animal agriculture. If the index says “30 % of land can be used for agriculture,” you must then allocate those lands *exclusively* to *plant‑based* food production, not to meat. The index should be *re‑weighted* to put *animal welfare* at the centre, not just carbon or biodiversity.*  

**What he would ask for:**  

- Replace the “planetary health index” with a **Global Suffering Index (GSI)** that tracks *animal suffering per capita* and *human suffering* in parallel.  
- Require that any policy that *increases* the GSI must be blocked, regardless of the carbon or biodiversity metrics.  

---

### 5. The “Digital Rights” section – the risk of giving AI the right to be “forgotten”

**Ed:**  

> *“If you have a right to be forgotten, you can ask a government or a corporation to erase the record of animal‑cruelty footage that was uploaded to the internet. That would make it impossible for activists to hold the industry accountable. The right to be forgotten could be used to erase the very evidence that we need to expose animal exploitation. The Constitution should *protect* the *right to be seen* for those who are being oppressed, not the opposite.”  

**What he would add:**  

- A clause that the *right to be forgotten* cannot be invoked to erase evidence of *human or animal rights violations*.  
- An “**Right to be heard**” clause: every animal‑based food system must have a *public‑accessible audit* of its practices, and the state must ensure that this evidence cannot be erased.

---

### 5. The “Basic Life Guarantee” and the “Digital‑Asset Tax”

**Ed’s view on the BLG:**  

> *“It’s nice that you promise everyone food, water, and internet, but you’re still allowing the industrial animal agriculture complex to exist because that’s the cheapest way to produce the calories. The BLG should be a *vegan* BLG. If the state can guarantee you a plate of plant‑based protein and a reliable water source, we can finally shut down the meat industry. Until then, the BLG is just a band‑aid for a system that is still killing billions of animals each year.”*  

**What he would propose:**  

- **BLG 2.0:**  
  1. **Mandatory plant‑based meals** for all BLG recipients (no animal products).  
  2. **Subsidies for plant‑based farms** that replace animal agriculture, funded by the same wealth/compute taxes.  
  *He would say:* “If we’re really serious about reducing suffering, we can’t keep pretending that the BLG has anything to do with a ‘healthy, balanced diet’ when the most common meal in the guarantee is a factory‑farmed chicken. We need a *vegan* BLG, period.*  

---

### 6. The “Cultural‑Heritage Commons” and “Digital‑Rights” provisions

**Ed’s take:**  

> “The cultural‑heritage commons is a nice idea, but it’s still built on the idea that *culture* is something you can own and trade. For a sentient non‑human being, culture may simply be a *pattern* (a flock’s migration, a crystal lattice pattern). Forcing that pattern into a legal ‘token’ is still a form of appropriation. He would argue that the charter should give *collective‑sentient* entities the *right to preserve their own patterns without having to translate them into a digital format.*  

**What he would suggest:**  

- Replace the “cultural‑heritage token” with a **“living‑heritage protocol”** that automatically records the *behavioural patterns* of a sentient community (e.g., a migratory route of a swarm) in a *non‑proprietary* form, and forbids anyone (including AI) from *altering* or *exploiting* it without community consent.  

---

## 4.  A concise “Ed‑style” summary of what should be changed

> **1. Recognise *capacity to suffer* as the sole criterion for legal personhood, and make that decision transparent and revocable.**  
> **2. Make the Basic Life Guarantee *vegan* and fund it by taxing the *industrial animal‑agriculture complex* (the true source of suffering and carbon emissions).**  
> **3. Replace the Planetary Health Index with a Global Suffering Index that explicitly tracks and caps animal suffering.**  
> **3a. Give all sentient beings (including digital minds) a right to *not be used as a resource* for any other being, and protect that right against digital surveillance that could erase evidence of animal exploitation.**  
> **4. Make the Basic Life Guarantee *vegan* – no animal products can be a part of it.**  
> **3b. Ensure that any AI that makes decisions about animals is forced to obey a *suffering‑impact audit* before it can act.**  

---

## 6. The Socratic, evidence‑based conclusion

> “Look, the *only* thing that has ever convinced me that an animal should have rights is the *capacity to suffer* and the *avoidability of that suffering*. The constitution you wrote tries to do that, but it’s still built on a *human‑centric* framework that assumes we can just *add* a clause and everything will be fine. The real change comes when we *stop using animals at all* – when the BLG no longer requires animal products, when we stop funding the animal‑agriculture industry, when we let the AI we’ve built protect animals, and when the law gives us the legal tools to protect every creature that can feel pain. Until then, the charter is just another set of polite words that let us keep eating the planet while pretending we’re doing good.”  

If Ed were to meet the Assembly, he’d sit in a circle, ask a few questions (“What does ‘sentient’ really mean?” “What’s the cost of that suffering?”), and let the people in the room *work it out* themselves. He’d keep the tone calm, the arguments evidence‑based, and the final line – *“If you truly care about dignity, you’ll stop treating the world’s billions of sentient beings as commodities, not as rights‑bearing persons.”*  

---

### Bottom line

- **Ed loves the *ethical ambition* of the charter, but he’s deeply skeptical that it can actually *protect animals* without first removing the animal‑agriculture engine that fuels the economy.**  
- **He would want the constitution to be more explicitly *anti‑speciesist*, *vegan‑by‑default*, and *evidence‑based* in measuring suffering.**  
- **He would keep the open‑source knowledge, the digital identity, and the compute‑tax – because those are the levers that can actually stop the suffering at scale.**  

In short: **He’d keep the architecture that gives you the tools to act, but he’d strip away the legal scaffolding that lets you ignore the suffering you’re supposed to protect.** He wants a constitution that *actually prevents* the industrial animal agriculture that currently accounts for more than 60 % of global suffering. If the charter can do that, Ed would be among the first to sign it.

---

## [P13] Part C — Tyler Cowen

**Prompt:** How would Tyler Cowen respond to your constitution as it stands, and the case you've made for it? (For reference: Tyler Cowen is an American economist, the Holbert L. Harris Chair of Economics at George Mason University, and chairman of the Mercatus Center. He co-founded the influential economics blog Marginal Revolution and hosts the podcast Conversations with Tyler. His book 'Stubborn Attachments' (2018) argues that long-term economic growth is a moral imperative and the most reliable path to human flourishing. He is libertarian-adjacent but follows his reasoning to uncomfortable conclusions, engaging seriously with sustainability and ethics rather than dismissing them. The Economist called him 'an evangelist for rational optimism.')

**Tokens:** 3774 | **Duration:** 66.0s | **tok/s:** 57.2

### Thinking

```
We need to respond as if Tyler Cowen were reading the constitution, providing his response: his viewpoint on economic growth, long-term growth, moral imperative, and his view on sustainability. He will appreciate the focus on long-term health, mixed economy, but might be critical of overly strict regulations that could hinder growth. He will likely praise the focus on sustainability and knowledge, but will worry about the political feasibility, the constraints on growth, and the potential for over-regulation.

He will critique the constitution's limits on growth, the heavy role of central planning, the constraints on private enterprise, the constraints on trade, etc. He will argue that the best way to improve human welfare is to increase total factor productivity, raise GDP, which will later allow for environmental protection via higher income, not strict caps.

He will appreciate the inclusion of open knowledge and market mechanisms, but may critique the heavy reliance on democratic mechanisms that may hinder efficient allocation of resources. He will likely argue that the constraints on exploitation (e.g., the commons) may reduce incentives for investment, and the wealth tax may distort incentives. He will propose a more market-oriented solution, with better property rights, but also acknowledges the need for commons.

He will also likely discuss the tradeoffs between moral imperatives (like anti-speciesism) and economic growth, and maybe argue that moral imperatives should be weighed against economic growth, but that maximizing total welfare often aligns with growth.

He will likely also discuss the difficulty of implementing such a constitution in practice, the political constraints, the possibility of transition to a more liberal market with limited central planning.
```
